<script>
	function goToTopic(topic) {
		var topicMap = applySpecialCharacterMapping(topic); 
		var url = "/xpl/topicbrowse.jsp?topic=" + escape(topicMap);
		window.top.location.href = url;
	}
</script>

		<!-- ngIf: ::vm.details.impactStatement || vm.details.abstract || vm.details.scope || vm.details.purpose  || vm.details.previewImage --><div ng-if="::vm.details.impactStatement || vm.details.abstract || vm.details.scope || vm.details.purpose  || vm.details.previewImage" ng-class="vm.details.graphicalAbstract ? 			<!-- ngIf: ::vm.details.abstract --><div ng-if="::vm.details.abstract" class="ng-scope">
				<strong>Abstract:</strong>
				<div class="abstract-text ng-binding" ng-bind-html="::vm.details.abstract">In this paper, we present the design and analysis of an energy-efficient 163-b elliptic curve cryptographic (ECC) processor suitable for passive ultrahigh frequency (UHF) radio frequency identification (RFID) tags that are usable for banknote authentication and anti-counterfeiting. Even partial public key cryptographic functionality has long been thought to consume too much power and to be too slow to be usable in passive UHF RFID systems. Utilizing a low-power design strategy with optimized register file management and an architecture based on the López-Dahab Algorithm, we designed a low-power ECC processor that is used with a modified ECC-DH authentication protocol. The ECC-DH authentication protocol is compatible with the ISO/IEC 18000-63 (“Gen2”) passive UHF RFID protocol. The ECC processor requires 12 145 gate equivalents. The ECC processor consumes 5.04 nJ/b at a frequency of 960 kHz when implemented in a 0.13-μm standard CMOS process. The tag identity authentication function requires 30 600 cycles to complete all scalar multiplication operations. This size, speed, and power of the ECC processor makes it practical to use within a passive UHF RFID tag and achieve up to 1500 banknote authentications per minute, which is sufficient for use in the fastest banknote counting machines.</div>
			</div><!-- end ngIf: ::vm.details.abstract -->

<div id="BodyWrapper" class="ArticlePage" xmlns:ieee="http://www.ieeexplore.ieee.org"><div id="article">
<div class="section" id="sec1"><div class="header article-hdr"><div class="kicker">		                        SECTION I.</div><h2>Introduction</h2></div><p>This Ultra High Frequency (UHF) Radio Frequency Identification (RFID) systems have made considerable progress since backscattering theory first appeared in 1948 <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref1" id="context_ref_1_1">[1]</a>. The large, multi-component, high-energy devices that intentionally generate a backscatter communication signal have been replaced by a single, ultra low-power microchip connected to a tuned antenna. The single microchip plus the antenna and packaging constitute a passive RFID tag that is capable of backscatter communication with an RFID reader. A passive RFID system consists of at least a tag, a reader and a data management system. One or more tags are attached to an object, and when that tagged object enters the communication zone of a reader, the modulated radio frequency (RF) signal transmitted by the reader is received by the tag. The tag harvests energy from the reader’s RF signal to power the microchip. The tag chip demodulates the RF signal, executes functionality in response to the signal and backscatters a response according to the message in the signal. The reader sends all tag responses to the data management system where useful functionality is performed.</p><p>Low cost passive RFID systems are widely used in a broad, range of applications, such as logistics, retail sales, just-in-time manufacturing and electronic tolling, and they are being adopted for use in an ever growing number of new applications. In applications where personal, financial or critical data is communicated from the tag, the information transferred between the tag and the reader must be secured. Encrypted communications must be used to avoid private information leakage and severe economic and safety risks in these sensitive applications. Symmetric key encryption algorithms, such as AES (Advanced Encryption Standard), can be implemented on the low-power tag microchip within the size, power consumption and speed limitations typical of passive RFID tags <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref2" id="context_ref_2_1">[2]</a>, <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref3" id="context_ref_3_1">[3]</a>. Symmetric key algorithms require that both the reader and the tag share the same secret key; therefore, the use of symmetric key ciphers on tags used in large open systems presents a significant key management problem. In contrast, asymmetric ciphers, such as ECC (Elliptic Curve Cryptography), provide for simple key management by allowing for the public key for a tag to be widely and easily distributed while requiring only that the secret private key be stored and used only on the tag itself.</p><p>We note that the ECC enabled RFID tag can be used as an anti-counterfeit mechanism for an item that works in addition to the traditional physical anti-counterfeit mechanisms. The use of a unique private key on every tag makes it difficult for a counterfeiter to obtain a sufficiently large number of private keys to make counterfeit products, such as counterfeit bank notes, that are difficult to detect due to the reuse of the compromised private key. And, with sufficient physical protection of the memory storing the private key and the functions using the private key, it is prohibitively expensive for all but the most well funded attackers to retrieve a private key from a single RFID tag chip.</p><p>In this paper, we present the design of a low power (5.04 nJ/bit) ECC processor for a passive UHF RFID tag that is suitable for use in banknote authentication. Among the various secure asymmetric algorithms, ECC is the most usable for passive RFID systems due to its small key size. The smallest NIST (National Institute of Standards and Technology) recommended key size is only 163 bits <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref5" id="context_ref_5_1">[5]</a>. Juel and Pappu <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref6" id="context_ref_6_1">[6]</a> were the first to propose a practical RFID banknote authentication scheme which was later modified by Yang <i>et al.</i> <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref7" id="context_ref_7_1">[7]</a> to overcome various attacks. These schemes utilize an asymmetric algorithm to provide security. However, neither Juels and Pappu nor Yang <i>et al.</i> select a specific asymmetric algorithm for use. Consequently, neither provides a potential hardware design or implementation evaluation to evaluate the feasibility of their schemes. Tuyls and Batina present a basic ECC processor architecture for use with PUFs (Physically Unclonable Functions). However, Tuyls and Batina did not implement or synthesize their architecture; therefore, their size and power calculations are only estimates. Furthermore, none of these works were designed to work within the most common passive UHF RFID protocol, the EPC Gen2 air interface protocol <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref9" id="context_ref_9_1">[9]</a>. Our ECC processor design works with a modified ECC-DH authentication protocol that is compatible with the most recent EPC Gen2 air interface protocol, EPC Gen2v2.</p><p>The remainder of this paper is organized as follows. <a ref-type="sec" anchor="sec2" class="fulltext-link">Section II</a> presents and analyzes the design requirements for RFID enabled banknote authentication. We summarize previously published ECC implementations in <a ref-type="sec" anchor="sec3" class="fulltext-link">Section III</a>, and we summarize the relevant portions of the EPC Gen2v2 protocol in <a ref-type="sec" anchor="sec4" class="fulltext-link">Section IV</a>. In <a ref-type="sec" anchor="sec5" class="fulltext-link">Section V</a> we review the ECC algorithm, present the tag microchip architecture and demonstrate how the modified ECC-DH protocol works within the EPC Gen2v2 protocol. <a ref-type="sec" anchor="sec6" class="fulltext-link">Section VI</a> presents the hardware realization of the ECC processor highlighting the optimizations of the ALU (Arithmetic Logic Unit) including the multiplication, squaring, modulo and addition operations and the key controlling module. In <a ref-type="sec" anchor="sec7" class="fulltext-link">Section VII</a> we present the simulation results of our ECC processor design through FPGA simulation and the synthesis results using <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="LaTeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-1-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-1" role="math" style="width: 3.907em; display: inline-block;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 3.486em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.384em 1003.49em 2.646em -999.997em); top: -2.219em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-2"><span class="mn" id="MathJax-Span-3" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">0.13</span><span class="mtext" id="MathJax-Span-4" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;"> </span><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-5" style="font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">μ</span><span class="mtext" id="MathJax-Span-6" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">m</span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.225em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.33em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 1.137em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-1">0.13~\mu \text{m}</script>
</tex-math></inline-formula> CMOS process. We draw the relevant conclusions in <a ref-type="sec" anchor="sec8" class="fulltext-link">Section VIII</a>.</p></div>
<div class="section" id="sec2"><div class="header article-hdr"><div class="kicker">		                        SECTION II.</div><h2>Requirement Analysis</h2></div><p>A passive UHF RFID tag is an ultra-low-power device that operates in the 860-960 MHz frequency range. A passive RFID tag harvests all of its operating energy from electromagnetic waves incident upon its antenna and communicates through backscatter, a form of modulated reflection, communication. Passive UHF RFID systems provide for long communication ranges, up to 30 m today, between a reader and a simple identity only tag <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref10" id="context_ref_10_2">[10]</a>. However, the required communication range for secure tags is typically significantly shorter with ECC-enabled banknote tags requiring only up to 20 cm communication range. With a communication frequency of 900 MHz, 20 cm is at the boundary of the near field and the far field. Since most banknote communications will occur at less than 20 cm, we utilize the near field model to analyze the power available to the tag. In general, the power received by the RFID tag chip can be expressed using <a ref-type="disp-formula" anchor="deqn1-3" href="#deqn1-3" class="fulltext-link">Eq.1</a> with its components as defined in <a ref-type="disp-formula" anchor="deqn1-3" href="#deqn1-3" class="fulltext-link">Eq.2</a> and <a ref-type="disp-formula" anchor="deqn1-3" href="#deqn1-3" class="fulltext-link">Eq.3</a>.<disp-formula id="deqn1-3" class="display-formula"><tex-math notation="LaTeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><div class="MathJax_Display"><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-2-Frame" tabindex="0" style="width: 100%;"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-7" role="math" style="width: 100%; top: -1.318em; left: 0.003em;"><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: 0.003em; border-top: 1.3px solid; width: 1.745em; height: 0px;"></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 1.084em;"></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-174" style="vertical-align: 0.003em;"><span style="font-family: MathJax_Size3;">)</span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span><span style="position: absolute; top: -5.222em; left: 3.426em;"><span class="texatom" id="MathJax-Span-175"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-176"><span class="mn" id="MathJax-Span-177" style="font-size: 70.7%; font-family: MathJax_Main;">2</span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span></span></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 9.072em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; position: absolute; width: 1.264em; height: 0px; clip: rect(-4.441em 1001.2em 3.306em -999.997em); top: 0.003em; right: 0.003em; margin-right: 0.003em;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(3.126em 1001.2em 4.447em -999.997em); top: -7.565em; right: 0.003em;"><span class="mtd" id="mjx-eqn-1"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-11"><span class="mtext" id="MathJax-Span-12" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">(1)</span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(3.126em 1001.2em 4.447em -999.997em); top: -3.3em; right: 0.003em;"><span class="mtd" id="mjx-eqn-2"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-143"><span class="mtext" id="MathJax-Span-144" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">(2)</span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(3.126em 1001.2em 4.447em -999.997em); top: -1.138em; right: 0.003em;"><span class="mtd" id="mjx-eqn-3"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-148"><span class="mtext" id="MathJax-Span-149" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">(3)</span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span></span></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 9.192em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -4.33em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 9.203em;"></span></span></nobr></span></div><script type="math/tex; mode=display" id="MathJax-Element-2">\begin{align} P_{chip}=&amp;P_{reader} \rho C\tau \\ \rho=&amp;\frac {4R_{r} R_{t}}{\left |{ {Z_{r} +Z_{t}} }\right |^{2}},\quad \tau =\frac {4R_{c} R_{a}}{\left |{ {Z_{c} +Z_{a}} }\right |^{2}},\quad C=G_{t} L_{path} G_{r} p\notag \\ \\ L_{path}=&amp;\left({\frac {\lambda }{4\pi d}}\right)^{2} \end{align}</script>
</tex-math><span class="formula"><span class="link">View Source</span><img aria-describedby="qtip-0" style="display:inline;" title="Right-click on figure or equation for MathML and additional features." data-hasqtip="0" class="qtooltip moreInfo" alt="Right-click on figure for MathML and additional features." src="/assets/img/icon.support.gif" border="0" height="20" width="24" /><span class="tex tex2jax_ignore" style="display:none;">\begin{align} P_{chip}=&amp;P_{reader} \rho C\tau \\ \rho=&amp;\frac {4R_{r} R_{t}}{\left |{ {Z_{r} +Z_{t}} }\right |^{2}},\quad \tau =\frac {4R_{c} R_{a}}{\left |{ {Z_{c} +Z_{a}} }\right |^{2}},\quad C=G_{t} L_{path} G_{r} p\notag \\ \\ L_{path}=&amp;\left({\frac {\lambda }{4\pi d}}\right)^{2} \end{align}
</span></span></disp-formula></p><p>For a reading distance of 20 cm at 900 MHz, we expect that a tag will have at least 1mW of incident power. This power level is easily achieved since the maximum radiated power for a reader is 1 W. However, given the potentially small reader and tag antenna that are likely to be used for banknotes, we take a conservative approach to the power requirement. A tag with chip sensitivity of -12 dBm (sensitivity between -18 dBm and -21 dBm is common today) will be able to harvest <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="LaTeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-3-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-178" role="math" style="width: 3.246em; display: inline-block;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 2.886em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.384em 1002.89em 2.646em -999.997em); top: -2.219em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-179"><span class="mn" id="MathJax-Span-180" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">63</span><span class="mtext" id="MathJax-Span-181" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;"> </span><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-182" style="font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">μ</span><span class="mtext" id="MathJax-Span-183" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">W</span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.225em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.33em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 1.137em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-3">63~\mu \text{W}</script>
</tex-math></inline-formula> for its chip operations from 1 mW of incident power. A typical Gen2 tag requires approximately <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="LaTeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-4-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-184" role="math" style="width: 3.246em; display: inline-block;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 2.886em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.384em 1002.89em 2.646em -999.997em); top: -2.219em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-185"><span class="mn" id="MathJax-Span-186" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">10</span><span class="mtext" id="MathJax-Span-187" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;"> </span><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-188" style="font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">μ</span><span class="mtext" id="MathJax-Span-189" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">W</span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.225em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.33em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 1.137em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-4">10~\mu \text{W}</script>
</tex-math></inline-formula> to operate without security <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref11" id="context_ref_11_2">[11]</a>. Therefore, the security operations must consume less than <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="LaTeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-5-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-190" role="math" style="width: 3.246em; display: inline-block;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 2.886em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.384em 1002.89em 2.646em -999.997em); top: -2.219em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-191"><span class="mn" id="MathJax-Span-192" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">53</span><span class="mtext" id="MathJax-Span-193" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;"> </span><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-194" style="font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">μ</span><span class="mtext" id="MathJax-Span-195" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">W</span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.225em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.33em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 1.137em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-5">53~\mu \text{W}</script>
</tex-math></inline-formula> of power.</p><p>Nowadays, the typical speed of banknote counting machines is between 900 and 1,000 notes per minute. Some specially designed machines work at speeds up 1,200 notes per minute. We set our design goal at a speed of 1,500 notes per minute to insure that even the fastest machines are accommodated by our design. This speed provides for 40ms for the complete communication between each tag and the reader.</p><p>We utilize a modified ECC-DH protocol as a one-way authentication protocol that supports tag authentication only. ECC-DH is a simple protocol that reduces the asymmetric encryption calculation overhead required on the tag. ECC-DH needs only one ECC encryption operation (and no inversion operations) to be performed on the tag. Thus, the ECC encryption operation must take no more than 40 ms to complete as to allow for reasonable communications.</p></div>
<div class="section" id="sec3"><div class="header article-hdr"><div class="kicker">		                        SECTION III.</div><h2>ECC Related Work</h2></div><p>Low-power and compact implementations of ECC processors have been explored well beyond the López-Dahab Algorithm. Optimized implementations have been proposed since at least the mid-1980’s <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref15" id="context_ref_15_3">[15]</a>, <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref16" id="context_ref_16_3">[16]</a>. Interest in implementations amenable to passive UHF RFID systems has arisen more recently. Lee <i>et al.</i> <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref17" id="context_ref_17_3">[17]</a> propose a reduced register ECC processor that utilizes redundant modular operations to achieve performance usable with RFID systems. Using a <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="LaTeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-6-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-196" role="math" style="width: 3.907em; display: inline-block;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 3.486em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.384em 1003.49em 2.646em -999.997em); top: -2.219em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-197"><span class="mn" id="MathJax-Span-198" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">0.13</span><span class="mtext" id="MathJax-Span-199" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;"> </span><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-200" style="font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">μ</span><span class="mtext" id="MathJax-Span-201" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">m</span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.225em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.33em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 1.137em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-6">0.13~\mu \text{m}</script>
</tex-math></inline-formula> CMOS process, the synthesis shows that their design requires less than 12,500 Gate Equivalents (GEs) and consumes 12.08 <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="LaTeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-7-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-202" role="math" style="width: 1.805em; display: inline-block;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 1.625em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.384em 1001.63em 2.646em -999.997em); top: -2.219em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-203"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-204" style="font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">μ</span><span class="mtext" id="MathJax-Span-205" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">W</span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.225em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.33em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 1.137em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-7">\mu \text{W}</script>
</tex-math></inline-formula>. In <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref18" id="context_ref_18_3">[18]</a>, the first hardware implementation of a binary Edwards curve is presented. The authors suggest the use of mixed <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="LaTeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-8-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-206" role="math" style="width: 0.664em; display: inline-block;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 0.604em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.565em 1000.6em 2.345em -999.997em); top: -2.159em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-207"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-208" style="font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">ω</span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.165em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.063em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 0.67em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-8">\omega </script>
</tex-math></inline-formula>-<i>coordinates</i>with the common <i>Z – coordinates</i>to reduce the size of the register file. The design is realized in 13,427 GEs and takes 149.5 ms to finish computation when clocked at 400 Hz. Batina <i>et al.</i> <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref19" id="context_ref_19_3">[19]</a> evaluate HECC and ECC over composite fields and reduce the register number to reduce gate count. Their results show the combination of two fields will reduce the size of the ALU module while the memory required will be slightly bigger. Kumar and Paar <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref20" id="context_ref_20_3">[20]</a> present an area optimized ECC processor over a binary field. Inversion operations and fast square modules are implemented in the design with affine coordinates. An area between 10,000 and 18,000 GEs on a <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="LaTeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-9-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-209" role="math" style="width: 3.907em; display: inline-block;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 3.486em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.384em 1003.49em 2.646em -999.997em); top: -2.219em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-210"><span class="mn" id="MathJax-Span-211" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">0.35</span><span class="mtext" id="MathJax-Span-212" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;"> </span><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-213" style="font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">μ</span><span class="mtext" id="MathJax-Span-214" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">m</span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.225em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.33em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 1.137em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-9">0.35~\mu \text{m}</script>
</tex-math></inline-formula> CMOS process is achieved. Hein <i>et al.</i> <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref21" id="context_ref_21_3">[21]</a> present a fully functional ECC enabled RFID implementation that can fulfill the requirements of passive HF RFID tags compatible with the ISO-18000-3-1air interface standard. The power consumption obtained by measurement is <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="LaTeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-10-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-215" role="math" style="width: 4.087em; display: inline-block;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 3.667em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.384em 1003.67em 2.646em -999.997em); top: -2.219em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-216"><span class="mn" id="MathJax-Span-217" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">8.57</span><span class="mtext" id="MathJax-Span-218" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;"> </span><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-219" style="font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">μ</span><span class="mtext" id="MathJax-Span-220" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">W</span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.225em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.33em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 1.137em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-10">8.57~\mu \text{W}</script>
</tex-math></inline-formula> at 106 kHz. Bock <i>et al.</i> <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref22" id="context_ref_22_3">[22]</a> present a challenge response protocol similar to ECC-DH for tag authentication. Their 163-bit ECC engine is less than 0.8 mm2 in a 220 nm CMOS technology. The energy consumed is <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="LaTeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-11-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-221" role="math" style="width: 3.246em; display: inline-block;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 2.886em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.384em 1002.89em 2.646em -999.997em); top: -2.219em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-222"><span class="mn" id="MathJax-Span-223" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">79</span><span class="mtext" id="MathJax-Span-224" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;"> </span><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-225" style="font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">μ</span><span class="mtext" id="MathJax-Span-226" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">W</span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.225em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.33em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 1.137em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-11">79~\mu \text{W}</script>
</tex-math></inline-formula> at 847 kHz. Ting <i>et al.</i> <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref23" id="context_ref_23_3">[23]</a> designed a very constrained device by implementing a scheduling of atomic operations. The architecture of the ALU and the use of a circular shift based register file realizes the scheduling effectively. By using 65 nm process, their design is implemented in 11,831 GEs and consumes <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="LaTeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-12-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-227" role="math" style="width: 4.087em; display: inline-block;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 3.667em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.384em 1003.67em 2.646em -999.997em); top: -2.219em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-228"><span class="mn" id="MathJax-Span-229" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">4.50</span><span class="mtext" id="MathJax-Span-230" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;"> </span><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-231" style="font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">μ</span><span class="mtext" id="MathJax-Span-232" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">W</span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.225em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.33em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 1.137em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-12">4.50~\mu \text{W}</script>
</tex-math></inline-formula> with a clock frequency of 140 kHz.</p><p>These ECC related works utilize a range of methods amenable to passive RFID tags. However, each consumes either too much power, too much area or too much time to meet the requirements for banknote machines. The work presented in this paper focuses on designing a highly energy-efficient ECC processor suitable for banknote machines. Our presented design utilizes various low power design strategies to reduce power consumption at the expense of a slightly larger area. Additionally, our register file management is optimized for low power operation, and it has better performance than the shift register method proposed in the related works. The execution time of the baseband integrated with our processor meets the requirement of the Gen2v2 protocol and the banknote machine.</p></div>
<div class="section" id="sec4"><div class="header article-hdr"><div class="kicker">		                        SECTION IV.</div><h2>The EPC Gen2 PRotocol</h2></div><p>The EPC Gen2 air interface protocol standard was first published in 2004 <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref24" id="context_ref_24_4">[24]</a>. It defines the physical and logical requirements, including the Physical layer and the Link layer, for a passive UHF RFID system. The security enhanced version of Gen2, Gen2v2, was ratified in 2013 and was the first major update to the protocol since 2008.</p><p>The Gen2 protocol was designed for the field of retail supply chain. But in recent years, its use has been extended to applications including driver’s license and access control <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref25" id="context_ref_25_4">[25]</a>. The security and privacy requirements of these new applications led to the development of the Gen2v2 protocol.</p><p>The primary functionality of the Gen2 protocol involves the singulation and identification of tags within a reader’s communication zone. This identification process utilizes a framed slotted Aloha anti-collision protocol to first singulate a tag and then retrieve the unique identifier stored within the tag.</p><p>The basic identification process utilizes four commands as defined in <a ref-type="table" anchor="table1" class="fulltext-link">Table I</a>. The <i>Select</i> and <i>Query</i> commands select a subset of the tags to participate in the identification process and then begin the identification process respectively. A tag, upon receiving the <i>Query</i> command, randomly selects a slot number from within the range specified by the Query command. For every <i>QueryRep</i> command received, the tag decrements its counter by 1. When a tag’s counter reaches zero, the tag communicates a 16-bit random number (RN16) to the reader. The reader, upon receiving an RN16, sends the <i>ACK</i> command with the received RN16 as the command payload. The tag, upon receiving an <i>ACK</i> command with its just sent RN16, communicates its entire stored unique identifier to the reader. </p><div class="figure figure-full table" id="table1"><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">TABLE 1 </b>
Primary Gen2 Identification Commands<div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></div><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/6287639/7859429/7583685/ota.t1-2615003-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/6287639/7859429/7583685/ota.t1-2615003-small.gif" alt="Table 1" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div></div><p></p><p>When the tag communicates its identifier to the reader, the tag is singulated, meaning that only that tag is communicating with the reader. From this point, the reader may issue commands directly to the tag with all other tags ignoring the sent commands. Once the reader has completed its communications, if any, with only the singulated tag, the reader issues a <i>QueryRep</i> command to move to the next slot in the identification process.</p><p>The Gen2v2 protocol adds security and file management in a standardized manner to the Gen2 protocol while being fully backward-compatible to the original Gen2 protocol. Gen2v2 includes new security features and security functionalities including:
</p><ul style="list-style-type:disc"><li><p>Untraceable function to hide portions of memory.</p></li><li><p>Cryptographic authentication of tags and readers to reduce risk of counterfeiting and unauthorized tag access.</p></li><li><p>Enhanced User Memory for supplementary encoding and file access.</p></li><li><p>Non removable flag to indicate that a tag has been removed from its original packaging.</p></li></ul><p></p><p>Authentication is the primary security function for Gen2v2 compliant tags. Tag authentication allows the reader or information system to authenticate the identity of the tag while reader authentication allows the tag to authenticate the reader prior to allowing access to the tag’s resources. Authentication begins with the <i>Authenticate</i> command issued to a singulated tag. The first <i>Authenticate</i> command is used to authenticate the tag to the reader or information system. For banknote authentication, only tag authentication is required in an embedded tag.</p><p>The Gen2v2 protocol utilizes a new kind of function called <i>in-process reply</i>. An in-process tag reply is a reply that meets T5 time restriction rather than the much more stringent T<sub>1</sub> time restriction. The in-process reply allows the tag to indicate to the reader that it is continuing its execution of a command but is not yet completed. The in-process reply also allows the reader to be certain that the tag is still singulated and powered. The T<sub>5</sub> timing constraint for the in-process reply allows the tag to perform functions that require a relatively large amount of time (10’s of milliseconds). The in-process reply is usable with the <i>Authenticate</i> command; therefore, it is possible to have cryptographic functions that take 10’s of milliseconds to compute. Therefore, the in-process reply of the Gen2v2 protocol greatly facilitates ECC processor design.</p></div>
<div class="section" id="sec5"><div class="header article-hdr"><div class="kicker">		                        SECTION V.</div><h2>System Architecture</h2></div><div class="section_2" id="sec5a"><h3>A. ECC Algorithm Security Analysis</h3><p>Elliptic curves that are most amenable for passive UHF RFID systems are defined over <i>GF(p)</i> and <i>GF(2<sup>n</sup></i>), where <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="LaTeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-13-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-233" role="math" style="width: 0.544em; display: inline-block;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 0.483em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.565em 1000.48em 2.526em -999.997em); top: -2.159em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-234"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-235" style="font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">p</span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.165em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.263em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 0.87em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-13">p </script>
</tex-math></inline-formula> is a prime number and <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="LaTeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-14-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-236" role="math" style="width: 0.664em; display: inline-block;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 0.604em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.565em 1000.6em 2.345em -999.997em); top: -2.159em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-237"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-238" style="font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">n</span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.165em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.063em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 0.67em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-14">n</script>
</tex-math></inline-formula> is the order of the irreducible polynomial. Both fields have their own advantages when providing the same level of security based on the Elliptic Curve Discrete Logarithm Problem (ECDLP). The <i>GF(p)</i> field enables simple squaring operations in ECC scalar multiplication by simply shifting the operand, while the <i>GF(2</i><inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="LaTeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-15-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-239" role="math" style="width: 1.024em; display: inline-block;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 0.904em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.324em 1000.78em 2.646em -999.997em); top: -2.219em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-240"><span class="msubsup" id="MathJax-Span-241"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 0.483em; height: 0px;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(3.847em 1000em 4.207em -999.997em); top: -4.021em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-242"></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span><span style="position: absolute; top: -4.381em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="texatom" id="MathJax-Span-243"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-244"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-245" style="font-size: 70.7%; font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">n</span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span></span></span><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-246" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">)</span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.225em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.33em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 1.27em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-15">^{n}) </script>
</tex-math></inline-formula> field allows for a simple bit-XOR addition implementation. Due to the simple CMOS circuit realization of bit-XOR, <i>GF(2</i><inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="LaTeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-16-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-247" role="math" style="width: 1.024em; display: inline-block;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 0.904em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.324em 1000.78em 2.646em -999.997em); top: -2.219em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-248"><span class="msubsup" id="MathJax-Span-249"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 0.483em; height: 0px;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(3.847em 1000em 4.207em -999.997em); top: -4.021em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-250"></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span><span style="position: absolute; top: -4.381em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="texatom" id="MathJax-Span-251"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-252"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-253" style="font-size: 70.7%; font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">n</span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span></span></span><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-254" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">)</span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.225em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.33em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 1.27em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-16">^{n})</script>
</tex-math></inline-formula> is usually chosen for low-cost designs.</p><p>The most important operation of ECC is elliptic curve scalar multiplication, i.e., <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="LaTeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-17-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-255" role="math" style="width: 11.174em; display: inline-block;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 10.033em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.324em 1010.03em 3.967em -999.997em); top: -2.219em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-256"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-257" style="font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">k</span><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-258" style="font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">P<span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; height: 1px; width: 0.123em;"></span></span><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-259" style="font-family: MathJax_Main; padding-left: 0.303em;">=</span><span class="munderover" id="MathJax-Span-260" style="padding-left: 0.303em;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 7.39em; height: 0px;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(3.186em 1007.39em 4.267em -999.997em); top: -4.021em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-264"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-265" style="font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">P<span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; height: 1px; width: 0.123em;"></span></span><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-266" style="font-family: MathJax_Main; padding-left: 0.243em;">+</span><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-267" style="font-family: MathJax_Math-italic; padding-left: 0.243em;">P<span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; height: 1px; width: 0.123em;"></span></span><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-268" style="font-family: MathJax_Main; padding-left: 0.243em;">+</span><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-269" style="font-family: MathJax_Main; padding-left: 0.243em;">⋅</span><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-270" style="font-family: MathJax_Main; padding-left: 0.243em;">⋅</span><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-271" style="font-family: MathJax_Main; padding-left: 0.243em;">⋅</span><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-272" style="font-family: MathJax_Main; padding-left: 0.243em;">+</span><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-273" style="font-family: MathJax_Math-italic; padding-left: 0.243em;">P<span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; height: 1px; width: 0.123em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.685em 1007.39em 2.646em -999.997em); top: -1.619em; left: -0.117em;"><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-274" style=""><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 7.39em; top: -4.021em; left: 3.246em;"><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span><span style="font-family: MathJax_Size4; position: absolute; top: -4.021em; left: 0.423em;"><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span><span style="font-family: MathJax_Size4; position: absolute; top: -4.021em; left: 0.784em;"><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span><span style="font-family: MathJax_Size4; position: absolute; top: -4.021em; left: 1.144em;"><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span><span style="font-family: MathJax_Size4; position: absolute; top: -4.021em; left: 1.444em;"><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span><span style="font-family: MathJax_Size4; position: absolute; top: -4.021em; left: 1.805em;"><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span><span style="font-family: MathJax_Size4; position: absolute; top: -4.021em; left: 2.165em;"><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span><span style="font-family: MathJax_Size4; position: absolute; top: -4.021em; left: 2.526em;"><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span><span style="font-family: MathJax_Size4; position: absolute; top: -4.021em; left: 2.886em;"><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span><span style="font-family: MathJax_Size4; position: absolute; top: -4.021em; left: 4.087em;"><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span><span style="font-family: MathJax_Size4; position: absolute; top: -4.021em; left: 4.447em;"><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span><span style="font-family: MathJax_Size4; position: absolute; top: -4.021em; left: 4.808em;"><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span><span style="font-family: MathJax_Size4; position: absolute; top: -4.021em; left: 5.168em;"><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span><span style="font-family: MathJax_Size4; position: absolute; top: -4.021em; left: 5.529em;"><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span><span style="font-family: MathJax_Size4; position: absolute; top: -4.021em; left: 5.829em;"><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span><span style="font-family: MathJax_Size4; position: absolute; top: -4.021em; left: 6.189em;"><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span><span style="font-family: MathJax_Size4; position: absolute; top: -4.021em; left: 6.55em;"><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.225em;"></span></span></span></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(3.366em 1002.28em 4.327em -999.997em); top: -2.58em; left: 2.526em;"><span class="texatom" id="MathJax-Span-275"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-276"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-277" style="font-size: 70.7%; font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">k</span><span class="mtext" id="MathJax-Span-278" style="font-size: 70.7%; font-family: MathJax_Main;"> </span><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-279" style="font-size: 70.7%; font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">t</span><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-280" style="font-size: 70.7%; font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">i</span><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-281" style="font-size: 70.7%; font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">m</span><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-282" style="font-size: 70.7%; font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">e</span><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-283" style="font-size: 70.7%; font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">s</span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.225em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -1.797em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 2.67em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-17">kP=\underbrace {P+P+\cdot \cdot \cdot +P}_{k ~times}</script>
</tex-math></inline-formula>, where <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="LaTeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-18-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-284" role="math" style="width: 0.904em; display: inline-block;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 0.784em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.324em 1000.78em 2.345em -999.997em); top: -2.159em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-285"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-286" style="font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">P<span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; height: 1px; width: 0.123em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.165em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.063em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 0.87em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-18">P</script>
</tex-math></inline-formula> is the 4 base point on the elliptic curve and k is the scalar operand. Scalar multiplication can be divided into sub-operations (point addition and point doubling) using the Montgomery Ladder algorithm which prevents simple power analysis attacks <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref12" id="context_ref_12_5a">[12]</a>. The point operations are realized by finite field operations: addition, multiplication, squaring and division.</p><p>The López-Dahab Algorithm <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref14" id="context_ref_14_5a">[14]</a> is an optimized Montgomery algorithm that minimizes on-tag ECC functionality and allows the tag to compute efficiently elliptic scalar multiplication in a hardware-restricted environment. The compute intensive operations are performed by the reader.</p><p>The method has two versions based on affine-coordinates (LD2A) and projective-coordinates (LD2P) respectively <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref13" id="context_ref_13_5a">[13]</a>. In this work, LD2P is adopted since algorithm LD2P is immune against timing attack. Because in <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref14" id="context_ref_14_5a">[14]</a>, step 4 executes the <i>M add</i> and <i>M double</i> in both cases of <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="LaTeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-19-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-287" role="math" style="width: 3.006em; display: inline-block;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 2.706em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.324em 1002.65em 2.586em -999.997em); top: -2.219em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-288"><span class="msubsup" id="MathJax-Span-289"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 0.844em; height: 0px;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(3.126em 1000.48em 4.207em -999.997em); top: -4.021em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-290" style="font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">k</span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span><span style="position: absolute; top: -3.901em; left: 0.544em;"><span class="texatom" id="MathJax-Span-291"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-292"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-293" style="font-size: 70.7%; font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">i</span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span></span></span><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-294" style="font-family: MathJax_Main; padding-left: 0.303em;">=</span><span class="mn" id="MathJax-Span-295" style="font-family: MathJax_Main; padding-left: 0.303em;">1</span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.225em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.263em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 1.07em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-19">k_{i} = 1</script>
</tex-math></inline-formula> and <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="LaTeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-20-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-296" role="math" style="width: 3.006em; display: inline-block;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 2.706em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.324em 1002.65em 2.586em -999.997em); top: -2.219em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-297"><span class="msubsup" id="MathJax-Span-298"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 0.844em; height: 0px;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(3.126em 1000.48em 4.207em -999.997em); top: -4.021em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-299" style="font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">k</span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span><span style="position: absolute; top: -3.901em; left: 0.544em;"><span class="texatom" id="MathJax-Span-300"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-301"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-302" style="font-size: 70.7%; font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">i</span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span></span></span><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-303" style="font-family: MathJax_Main; padding-left: 0.303em;">=</span><span class="mn" id="MathJax-Span-304" style="font-family: MathJax_Main; padding-left: 0.303em;">0</span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.225em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.263em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 1.07em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-20">k_{i} = 0</script>
</tex-math></inline-formula>, the algorithm does not depend on the <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="LaTeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-21-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-305" role="math" style="width: 0.964em; display: inline-block;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 0.844em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.264em 1000.84em 2.526em -999.997em); top: -2.159em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-306"><span class="msubsup" id="MathJax-Span-307"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 0.844em; height: 0px;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(3.126em 1000.48em 4.207em -999.997em); top: -4.021em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-308" style="font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">k</span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span><span style="position: absolute; top: -3.901em; left: 0.544em;"><span class="texatom" id="MathJax-Span-309"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-310"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-311" style="font-size: 70.7%; font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">i</span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.165em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.263em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 1.07em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-21">k_{i}</script>
</tex-math></inline-formula> value, it has no secret key dependency nor executing procedure of cryptographic transaction <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref13" id="context_ref_13_5a">[13]</a>.</p></div><div class="section_2" id="sec5b"><h3>B. Baseband Architecture</h3><p>This ECC processor can be integrated into an RFID digital baseband compliant with the Gen2v2 protocol shown in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig1" class="fulltext-link">Fig.1</a>.
</p><div class="figure figure-full" id="fig1"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/6287639/7859429/7583685/ota1-2615003-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/6287639/7859429/7583685/ota1-2615003-small.gif" alt="Figure 1" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">FIGURE 1. </b><fig><p>ECC tag microchip baseband architecture.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/7583685/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div><p></p><p>The baseband module functions are shown in <a ref-type="table" anchor="table2" class="fulltext-link">Table II</a>. The Tag Front End module detects the signal from the reader and backscatter modulates the tag’s response. It also harvests power for the chip from the signal incident on the antenna. The Demod module translates the digital signal to the original bit stream according to the coding scheme (e.g., FM0). The Cyclic Redundancy Check module checks whether the message received is without transmission errors. Only error free messages are processed. The Decode module analyzes the message on the basis of EPC Gen2v2 protocol to get the command from the message. PWM is a power module consisting of several clocks set at different frequencies to meet various requirements of the tag modules. Reducing module frequency can reduce power consumption to a large extent. The PRNG, Secure Engine and RROM work together to deal with arithmetic computation and generate the tag response. The Mod module modulates the signal as an opposite process to Demod. At last, the final output is transferred back to the reader via the Tag Frontend and OCU.</p><div class="figure figure-full table" id="table2"><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">TABLE 2 </b>
Baseband Module<div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></div><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/6287639/7859429/7583685/ota.t2-2615003-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/6287639/7859429/7583685/ota.t2-2615003-small.gif" alt="Table 2" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div></div><p></p></div><div class="section_2" id="sec5c"><h3>C. ECC-DH Protocol</h3><p>Elliptic curve cryptography has been the basis of many cryptographic protocols for authentication and key agreement. The first protocol is due to Diffie and Hellmann <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref26" id="context_ref_26_5c">[26]</a>, which is described in <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref27" id="context_ref_27_5c">[27]</a> as a key agreement method between two entities based on <i>GF(p)</i>.</p><p>The ECC-DH protocol is described in <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref28" id="context_ref_28_5c">[28]</a> in detail. Some of its commands are selected to construct a simple version in this paper. This version consists of the basic functions necessary for tag authentication. The authentication process is illustrated in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig2" class="fulltext-link">Fig.2</a>.
</p><div class="figure figure-full" id="fig2"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/6287639/7859429/7583685/ota2-2615003-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/6287639/7859429/7583685/ota2-2615003-small.gif" alt="Figure 2" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">FIGURE 2. </b><fig><p>Elliptic Curve static Diffie-Hellman authentication.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/7583685/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div><p></p><p>In the protocol, there are 3 commands: TAM 1.0, TAM 1.1, TAM 1.2. The tag has a static public/private key pair and a public key certificate. In a real application, the certificate should use a digital signature to bind the public key with the name of the organization that produced the key pair. During the certificate verification period, the reader should check whether the public key in the certificate is authentic by executing the signature verification algorithm. If the signature is invalid, the tag will not be accepted. In this version, we use an array of numbers to represent the certificate. The tag should give the right series of numbers back according to the reader’s command (TAM 1.1: Request Certificate). The reader has the authority to write a new certificate into the tag’s memory (TAM 1.0: Write the Certificate) if the tag is writable.</p><p>The detailed verification process (TAM 1.2) is displayed in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig3" class="fulltext-link">Fig.3</a>. The <i>rG, qT</i> and <i>rQ</i> operations are ECC scalar multiplications. After the reader verifies that the tag’s certificate is valid, it generates an ephemeral random number <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="LaTeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-22-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-312" role="math" style="width: 0.544em; display: inline-block;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 0.483em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.565em 1000.48em 2.345em -999.997em); top: -2.159em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-313"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-314" style="font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">r</span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.165em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.063em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 0.67em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-22">r</script>
</tex-math></inline-formula> and multiplies the base point <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="LaTeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-23-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-315" role="math" style="width: 0.904em; display: inline-block;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 0.784em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.264em 1000.78em 2.345em -999.997em); top: -2.159em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-316"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-317" style="font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">G</span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.165em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.063em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 0.937em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-23">G </script>
</tex-math></inline-formula> with this number. Then, the reader sends the temporary result <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="LaTeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-24-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-318" role="math" style="width: 0.844em; display: inline-block;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 0.724em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.324em 1000.72em 2.345em -999.997em); top: -2.159em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-319"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-320" style="font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">T<span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; height: 1px; width: 0.123em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.165em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.063em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 0.87em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-24">T</script>
</tex-math></inline-formula> to the tag. Upon receiving the challenge, the tag executes <i>qT</i>. The tag communicates the m result <i>qT<sub>x</sub></i> and <i>qT<sub>z</sub></i> back to the reader. The reader calculates the projective coordinates <i>(rQ)<sub>x</sub></i> <i>(qT)<sub>z</sub></i> and <i>(rQ)<sub>z</sub> (qT)</i><sub><i>x</i></sub> to check whether they are equal. If the response is correct, the reader accepts the tag.
</p><div class="figure figure-full" id="fig3"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/6287639/7859429/7583685/ota3-2615003-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/6287639/7859429/7583685/ota3-2615003-small.gif" alt="Figure 3" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">FIGURE 3. </b><fig><p>ECC Verification Procedure (TAM 1.2).</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/7583685/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div><p></p><p>In the whole communication process, the tag only needs to calculate one scalar multiplication and this operation is done in the projective coordinate without inversion or division. Therefore the computation overhead is quite low due to the short calculation time. In this design, we mainly focus on realizing TAM 1.1 and TAM 1.2.</p></div></div>
<div class="section" id="sec6"><div class="header article-hdr"><div class="kicker">		                        SECTION VI.</div><h2>Circuit Implementation</h2></div><p>The system architecture is shown in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig4" class="fulltext-link">Fig.4</a>. The ECC processor consists of 6 main modules described in the following. The ECC_FSM module controls the whole system according to a finite state machine. The REGISTER_FILE module has a 5*163 bit register array that is controlled by <i>reg_ select</i> and <i>swap</i>. The ALU module consists three finite field operation modules: FF_ADDER, FF_SQUARER and FF_MULT, mapping to addition, squaring and multiplication operation respectively.
</p><div class="figure figure-full" id="fig4"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/6287639/7859429/7583685/ota4-2615003-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/6287639/7859429/7583685/ota4-2615003-small.gif" alt="Figure 4" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">FIGURE 4. </b><fig><p>ECC processor system structure.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/7583685/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div><p></p><p>In <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig4" class="fulltext-link">Fig.4</a>, <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="LaTeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-25-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-321" role="math" style="width: 0.604em; display: inline-block;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 0.544em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.264em 1000.54em 2.345em -999.997em); top: -2.159em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-322"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-323" style="font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">k</span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.165em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.063em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 0.937em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-25">k</script>
</tex-math></inline-formula> represents the scalar key value; g refers to the x-coordinate of the base point in affine format received from the tag; <i>ecc_start</i> is the enable signal; <i>ecc_xa</i> and <i>ecc_za</i> are <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="LaTeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-26-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-324" role="math" style="width: 0.664em; display: inline-block;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 0.604em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.565em 1000.54em 2.345em -999.997em); top: -2.159em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-325"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-326" style="font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">x</span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.165em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.063em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 0.67em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-26">x </script>
</tex-math></inline-formula> and <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="LaTeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-27-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-327" role="math" style="width: 0.544em; display: inline-block;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 0.483em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.565em 1000.48em 2.345em -999.997em); top: -2.159em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-328"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-329" style="font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">z<span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; height: 1px; width: 0.003em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.165em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.063em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 0.67em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-27">z </script>
</tex-math></inline-formula> of the final output results sent back to the reader. Five 163-bit registers are used in the REGISTER_FILE module, and one intermediate register is used in the FF_MULT module in order to save chip area. The following sections explain the design of the modules in detail.</p><div class="section_2" id="sec6a"><h3>A. ALU</h3><p>Based on the Montgomery Ladder algorithm, the ECC processor can be divided into three primary modules: addition, multiplication and squaring.</p><div class="section_2" id="sec6a1"><h4>1) Addition</h4><p>In this work, non-supersingular elliptic curve over finite field and <i>GF</i>(<inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="LaTeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-28-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-330" role="math" style="width: 1.805em; display: inline-block;"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 1.625em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(1.144em 1001.63em 2.345em -999.997em); top: -2.159em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-331"><span class="msubsup" id="MathJax-Span-332"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 1.625em; height: 0px;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(3.186em 1000.42em 4.207em -999.997em); top: -4.021em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mn" id="MathJax-Span-333" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">2</span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span><span style="position: absolute; top: -4.441em; left: 0.483em;"><span class="texatom" id="MathJax-Span-334"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-335"><span class="mn" id="MathJax-Span-336" style="font-size: 70.7%; font-family: MathJax_Main;">163</span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 2.165em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; vertical-align: -0.063em; border-left: 0px solid; width: 0px; height: 1.07em;"></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-28">2^{163}</script>
</tex-math></inline-formula>) is used as the base <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref29" id="context_ref_29_6a1">[29]</a>. The elliptic curve can be represented by <a ref-type="disp-formula" anchor="deqn4" href="#deqn4" class="fulltext-link">(4)</a>.<disp-formula id="deqn4" class="display-formula"><tex-math notation="LaTeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview"></span><div class="MathJax_Display"><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-29-Frame" tabindex="0" style="width: 100%;"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-337" role="math" style="width: 100%; height: 0px;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(2.946em 1018.92em 4.447em -999.997em); top: -3.961em; left: 50%; margin-left: -9.486em;"><span class="mtd" id="MathJax-Span-343"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-344"><span class="msubsup" id="MathJax-Span-345"><span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; height: 1px; width: 0.003em;"></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span><span style="position: absolute; top: -4.441em; left: 0.544em;"><span class="texatom" id="MathJax-Span-347"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-348"><span class="mn" id="MathJax-Span-349" style="font-size: 70.7%; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span><span style="position: absolute; top: -4.441em; left: 0.604em;"><span class="texatom" id="MathJax-Span-356"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-357"><span class="mn" id="MathJax-Span-358" style="font-size: 70.7%; font-family: MathJax_Main;">3</span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span></span></span><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-359" style="font-family: MathJax_Main; padding-left: 0.243em;">+</span><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-360" style="font-family: MathJax_Math-italic; padding-left: 0.243em;">m</span><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-361" style="font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">x</span><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-362" style="font-family: MathJax_Main; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span><span style="position: absolute; top: -4.441em; left: 0.904em;"><span class="texatom" id="MathJax-Span-369"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-370"><span class="mn" id="MathJax-Span-371" style="font-size: 70.7%; position: relative; width: 1.024em; height: 0px;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(3.426em 1000.6em 4.207em -999.997em); top: -4.021em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-375" style="font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">n</span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span><span style="position: absolute; top: -4.441em; left: 0.604em;"><span class="texatom" id="MathJax-Span-376"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-377"><span class="mn" id="MathJax-Span-378" style="font-size: 70.7%; font-family: MathJax_Main;">2</span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span></span></span><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-379" style="font-family: MathJax_Main; padding-left: 0.303em;">≠</span><span class="mn" id="MathJax-Span-380" style="font-family: MathJax_Main; padding-left: 0.303em;">0</span><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-381" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">)</span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; position: absolute; width: 1.264em; height: 0px; clip: rect(-0.838em 1001.2em 0.483em -999.997em); top: 0.003em; right: 0.003em; margin-right: 0.003em;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(3.126em 1001.2em 4.447em -999.997em); top: -3.961em;
